\n
\n
\n\n
\nBREYER, J., DISSENTING
\nMCDONALD V. CHICAGO
\n561 U. S. ____ (2010)
\nSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES
\nNO. 08-1521
\n
\n

OTIS McDONALD, et\xc2\xa0al., PETITIONERS v. CITY OF CHICAGO, ILLINOIS, et\xc2\xa0al.

\n

on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the seventh circuit

\n

[June 28, 2010]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Breyer, with whom Justice Ginsburg and Justice Sotomayor join, dissenting.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In my view, Justice Stevens has demonstrated that the Fourteenth Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s guarantee of \xe2\x80\x9csubstantive due process\xe2\x80\x9d does not include a general right to keep and bear firearms for purposes of private self-defense. As he argues, the Framers did not write the Second Amendment with this objective in view. See ante, at 41\xe2\x80\x9344 (dissenting opinion). Unlike other forms of substantive liberty, the carrying of arms for that purpose often puts others\xe2\x80\x99 lives at risk. See ante, at 35\xe2\x80\x9337. And the use of arms for private self-defense does not warrant federal constitutional protection from state regulation. See ante, at 44\xe2\x80\x9351.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court, however, does not expressly rest its opinion upon \xe2\x80\x9csubstantive due process\xe2\x80\x9d concerns. Rather, it directs its attention to this Court\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cincorporation\xe2\x80\x9d precedents and asks whether the Second Amendment right to private self-defense is \xe2\x80\x9cfundamental\xe2\x80\x9d so that it applies to the States through the Fourteenth Amendment. See ante, at 11\xe2\x80\x9319.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I shall therefore separately consider the question of \xe2\x80\x9cincorporation.\xe2\x80\x9d I can find nothing in the Second Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s text, history, or underlying rationale that could warrant characterizing it as \xe2\x80\x9cfundamental\xe2\x80\x9d insofar as it seeks to protect the keeping and bearing of arms for private self-defense purposes. Nor can I find any justification for interpreting the Constitution as transferring ultimate regulatory authority over the private uses of firearms from democratically elected legislatures to courts or from the States to the Federal Government. I therefore conclude that the Fourteenth Amendment does not \xe2\x80\x9cincorporate\xe2\x80\x9d the Second Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s right \xe2\x80\x9cto keep and bear Arms.\xe2\x80\x9d And I consequently dissent.

\n

I

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Second Amendment says: \xe2\x80\x9cA well regulated Militia, being necessary to the security of a free State, the right of the people to keep and bear Arms, shall not be infringed.\xe2\x80\x9d Two years ago, in District of Columbia v. Heller, 554 U. S. ___ (2008), the Court rejected the pre-existing judicial consensus that the Second Amendment was primarily concerned with the need to maintain a \xe2\x80\x9cwell regulated Militia.\xe2\x80\x9d See id., at ___ (Stevens, J., dissenting) (slip op., at 2\xe2\x80\x933, and n.\xc2\xa02, 38\xe2\x80\x9345); United States v. Miller, 307 U. S. 174, 178 (1939). Although the Court acknowledged that \xe2\x80\x9cthe threat that the new Federal Government would destroy the citizens\xe2\x80\x99 militia by taking away their arms was the reason that right \xe2\x80\xa6 was codified in a written Constitution,\xe2\x80\x9d the Court asserted that \xe2\x80\x9cindividual self defense \xe2\x80\xa6 was the central component of the right itself.\xe2\x80\x9d Heller, supra, at ___ (slip op., at 26) (first emphasis added). The Court went on to hold that the Second Amendment restricted Congress\xe2\x80\x99 power to regulate handguns used for self-defense, and the Court found unconstitutional the District of Columbia\xe2\x80\x99s ban on the possession of handguns in the home. Id., at ___ (slip op., at 64).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court based its conclusions almost exclusively upon its reading of history. But the relevant history in Heller was far from clear: Four dissenting Justices disagreed with the majority\xe2\x80\x99s historical analysis. And subsequent scholarly writing reveals why disputed history provides treacherous ground on which to build decisions written by judges who are not expert at history.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Since Heller, historians, scholars, and judges have continued to express the view that the Court\xe2\x80\x99s historical account was flawed. See, e.g., Konig, Why the Second Amendment Has a Preamble: Original Public Meaning and the Political Culture of Written Constitutions in Revolutionary America, 56 UCLA L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 1295 (2009); Finkelman, It Really Was About a Well Regulated Militia, 59 Syracuse L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 267 (2008); P. Charles, The Second Amendment: The Intent and Its Interpretation by the States and the Supreme Court (2009); Merkel, The District of Columbia v. Heller and Antonin Scalia\xe2\x80\x99s Perverse Sense of Originalism, 13 Lewis & Clark L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 349 (2009); Kozuskanich, Originalism in a Digital Age: An Inquiry into the Right to Bear Arms, 29 J. Early Republic 585 (2009); Cornell, St. George Tucker\xe2\x80\x99s Lecture Notes, the Second Amendment, and Originalist Methodology, 103 Nw. U.\xc2\xa0L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 1541 (2009); Posner, In Defense of Looseness: The Supreme Court and Gun Control, New Republic, Aug. 27, 2008, pp. 32\xe2\x80\x9335; see also Epstein, A Structural Interpretation of the Second Amendment: Why Heller is (Probably) Wrong on Originalist Grounds, 59 Syracuse L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 171 (2008).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Consider as an example of these critiques an amici brief filed in this case by historians who specialize in the study of the English Civil Wars. They tell us that Heller misunderstood a key historical point. See Brief for English/Early American Historians as Amici Curiae (hereinafter English Historians\xe2\x80\x99 Brief) (filed by 21 professors at leading universities in the United States, United Kingdom, and Australia). Heller\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion that \xe2\x80\x9cindividual self-defense\xe2\x80\x9d was \xe2\x80\x9cthe central component\xe2\x80\x9d of the Second Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s right \xe2\x80\x9cto keep and bear Arms\xe2\x80\x9d rested upon its view that the Amendment \xe2\x80\x9ccodified a pre-existing right\xe2\x80\x9d that had \xe2\x80\x9cnothing whatever to do with service in a militia.\xe2\x80\x9d 554 U. S., at ___ (slip op., at 26, 19\xe2\x80\x9320). That view in turn rested in significant part upon Blackstone having described the right as \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98the right of having and using arms for self-preservation and defence,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d which reflected the provision in the English Declaration of Right of 1689 that gave the King\xe2\x80\x99s Protestant \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98subjects\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d the right to \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98have Arms for their defence suitable to their Conditions, and as allowed by law.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at ___ (slip op., at 19\xe2\x80\x9320) (quoting 1 W. Blackstone, Commentaries on the Laws of England 140 (1765) (hereinafter Blackstone) and 1 W. & M., c. 2, \xc2\xa77, in 3 Eng. Stat. at Large 441 (1689)). The Framers, said the majority, understood that right \xe2\x80\x9cas permitting a citizen to \xe2\x80\x98repe[l] force by force\xe2\x80\x99 when \xe2\x80\x98the intervention of society in his behalf, may be too late to prevent an injury.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 554 U. S., at ___ (slip op., at 21) (quoting St. George Tucker, 1 Blackstone\xe2\x80\x99s Commentaries 145\xe2\x80\x93146, n. 42 (1803)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The historians now tell us, however, that the right to which Blackstone referred had, not nothing, but everything, to do with the militia. As properly understood at the time of the English Civil Wars, the historians claim, the right to bear arms \xe2\x80\x9censured that Parliament had the power\xe2\x80\x9d to arm the citizenry: \xe2\x80\x9cto defend the realm\xe2\x80\x9d in the case of a foreign enemy, and to \xe2\x80\x9csecure the right of \xe2\x80\x98self-preservation,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cself-defense,\xe2\x80\x9d should \xe2\x80\x9cthe sovereign usurp the English Constitution.\xe2\x80\x9d English Historians\xe2\x80\x99 Brief 3, 8\xe2\x80\x9313, 23\xe2\x80\x9324 (emphasis added). Thus, the Declaration of Right says that private persons can possess guns only \xe2\x80\x9cas allowed by law.\xe2\x80\x9d See id., at 20\xe2\x80\x9324. Moreover, when Blackstone referred to \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98the right of having and using arms for self-preservation and defence,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d he was referring to the right of the people \xe2\x80\x9cto take part in the militia to defend their political liberties,\xe2\x80\x9d and to the right of Parliament (which represented the people) to raise a militia even when the King sought to deny it that power. Id., at 4, 24\xe2\x80\x9327 (emphasis added) (quoting 1 Blackstone 140). Nor can the historians find any convincing reason to believe that the Framers had something different in mind than what Blackstone himself meant. Compare Heller, supra, at ___ (slip op., at 21\xe2\x80\x9322) with English Historians\xe2\x80\x99 Brief 28\xe2\x80\x9340. The historians concede that at least one historian takes a different position, see id., at 7, but the Court, they imply, would lose a poll taken among professional historians of this period, say, by a vote of 8 to 1.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0If history, and history alone, is what matters, why would the Court not now reconsider Heller in light of these more recently published historical views? See Leegin Creative Leather Products, Inc. v. PSKS, Inc., 551 U. S. 877, 923\xe2\x80\x93924 (2007) (Breyer, J., dissenting) (noting that stare decisis interests are at their lowest with respect to recent and erroneous constitutional decisions that create unworkable legal regimes); Citizens United v. Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 558 U. S. ___, ___ (2010) (slip op., at 47) (listing similar factors); see also Wallace v. Jaffree, 472 U. S. 38, 99 (1985) (Rehnquist, J., dissenting) (\xe2\x80\x9c[S]tare decisis may bind courts as to matters of law, but it cannot bind them as to matters of history\xe2\x80\x9d). At the least, where Heller\xe2\x80\x99s historical foundations are so uncertain, why extend its applicability?

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0My aim in referring to this history is to illustrate the reefs and shoals that lie in wait for those nonexpert judges who place virtually determinative weight upon historical considerations. In my own view, the Court should not look to history alone but to other factors as well\xe2\x80\x94above all, in cases where the history is so unclear that the experts themselves strongly disagree. It should, for example, consider the basic values that underlie a constitutional provision and their contemporary significance. And it should examine as well the relevant consequences and practical justifications that might, or might not, warrant removing an important question from the democratic decisionmaking process. See ante, at 16\xe2\x80\x9320 (Stevens, J., dissenting) (discussing shortcomings of an exclusively historical approach).

\n

II

\n

A

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In my view, taking Heller as a given, the Fourteenth Amendment does not incorporate the Second Amendment right to keep and bear arms for purposes of private self-defense. Under this Court\xe2\x80\x99s precedents, to incorporate the private self-defense right the majority must show that the right is, e.g., \xe2\x80\x9cfundamental to the American scheme of justice,\xe2\x80\x9d Duncan v. Louisiana, 391 U. S. 145, 149 (1968); see ibid., n.\xc2\xa014; see also ante, at 44 (plurality opinion) (finding that the right is \xe2\x80\x9cfundamental\xe2\x80\x9d and therefore incorporated). And this it fails to do.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority here, like that in Heller, relies almost exclusively upon history to make the necessary showing. Ante, at 20\xe2\x80\x9333. But to do so for incorporation purposes is both wrong and dangerous. As Justice Stevens points out, our society has historically made mistakes\xe2\x80\x94for example, when considering certain 18th- and 19th-century property rights to be fundamental. Ante, at 19 (dissenting opinion). And in the incorporation context, as elsewhere, history often is unclear about the answers. See Part I, supra; Part III, infra.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Accordingly, this Court, in considering an incorporation question, has never stated that the historical status of a right is the only relevant consideration. Rather, the Court has either explicitly or implicitly made clear in its opinions that the right in question has remained fundamental over time. See, e.g., Apodaca v. Oregon, 406 U. S. 404, 410 (1972) (plurality opinion) (stating that the incorporation \xe2\x80\x9cinquiry must focus upon the function served\xe2\x80\x9d by the right in question in \xe2\x80\x9ccontemporary society\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)); Duncan v. Louisiana, 391 U. S. 145, 154 (1968) (noting that the right in question \xe2\x80\x9ccontinues to receive strong support\xe2\x80\x9d); Klopfer v. North Carolina, 386 U. S. 213, 226 (1967) (same). And, indeed, neither of the parties before us in this case has asked us to employ the majority\xe2\x80\x99s history-constrained approach. See Brief for Petitioners 67\xe2\x80\x9369 (arguing for incorporation based on trends in contemporary support for the right); Brief for Respondents City of Chicago et\xc2\xa0al. 23\xe2\x80\x9331 (hereinafter Municipal Respondents) (looking to current state practices with respect to the right).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I thus think it proper, above all where history provides no clear answer, to look to other factors in considering whether a right is sufficiently \xe2\x80\x9cfundamental\xe2\x80\x9d to remove it from the political process in every State. I would include among those factors the nature of the right; any contemporary disagreement about whether the right is fundamental; the extent to which incorporation will further other, perhaps more basic, constitutional aims; and the extent to which incorporation will advance or hinder the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s structural aims, including its division of powers among different governmental institutions (and the people as well). Is incorporation needed, for example, to further the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s effort to ensure that the government treats each individual with equal respect? Will it help maintain the democratic form of government that the Constitution foresees? In a word, will incorporation prove consistent, or inconsistent, with the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s efforts to create governmental institutions well suited to the carrying out of its constitutional promises?

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, I would take account of the Framers\xe2\x80\x99 basic reason for believing the Court ought to have the power of judicial review. Alexander Hamilton feared granting that power to Congress alone, for he feared that Congress, acting as judges, would not overturn as unconstitutional a popular statute that it had recently enacted, as legislators. The Federalist No. 78, p. 405 (G. Carey & J. McClellan eds. 2001) (A. Hamilton) (\xe2\x80\x9cThis independence of the judges is equally requisite to guard the constitution and the rights of individuals from the effects of those ill humours, which\xe2\x80\x9d can, at times, lead to \xe2\x80\x9cserious oppressions of the minor part in the community\xe2\x80\x9d). Judges, he thought, may find it easier to resist popular pressure to suppress the basic rights of an unpopular minority. See United States v. Carolene Products Co., 304 U. S. 144, 152, n.\xc2\xa04 (1938). That being so, it makes sense to ask whether that particular comparative judicial advantage is relevant to the case at hand. See, e.g., J. Ely, Democracy and Distrust (1980).

\n

B

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0How do these considerations apply here? For one thing, I would apply them only to the private self-defense right directly at issue. After all, the Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s militia-related purpose is primarily to protect States from federal regulation, not to protect individuals from militia-related regulation. Heller, 554 U. S., at ___ (slip op., at 26); see also Miller, 307 U. S., at 178. Moreover, the Civil War Amendments, the electoral process, the courts, and numerous other institutions today help to safeguard the States and the people from any serious threat of federal tyranny. How are state militias additionally necessary? It is difficult to see how a right that, as the majority concedes, has \xe2\x80\x9clargely faded as a popular concern\xe2\x80\x9d could possibly be so fundamental that it would warrant incorporation through the Fourteenth Amendment. Ante, at 22. Hence, the incorporation of the Second Amendment cannot be based on the militia-related aspect of what Heller found to be more extensive Second Amendment rights.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For another thing, as Heller concedes, the private self-defense right that the Court would incorporate has nothing to do with \xe2\x80\x9cthe reason\xe2\x80\x9d the Framers \xe2\x80\x9ccodified\xe2\x80\x9d the right to keep and bear arms \xe2\x80\x9cin a written Constitution.\xe2\x80\x9d 554 U. S., at ___ (slip op., at 26) (emphasis added). Heller immediately adds that the self-defense right was nonetheless \xe2\x80\x9cthe central component of the right.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. In my view, this is the historical equivalent of a claim that water runs uphill. See Part I, supra. But, taking it as valid, the Framers\xe2\x80\x99 basic reasons for including language in the Constitution would nonetheless seem more pertinent (in deciding about the contemporary importance of a right) than the particular scope 17th- or 18th-century listeners would have then assigned to the words they used. And examination of the Framers\xe2\x80\x99 motivation tells us they did not think the private armed self-defense right was of paramount importance. See Amar, The Bill of Rights as a Constitution, 100 Yale L.\xc2\xa0J. 1131, 1164 (1991) (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]o see the [Second] Amendment as primarily concerned with an individual right to hunt, or protect one\xe2\x80\x99s home,\xe2\x80\x9d would be \xe2\x80\x9clike viewing the heart of the speech and assembly clauses as the right of persons to meet to play bridge\xe2\x80\x9d); see also, e.g., Rakove, The Second Amendment: The Highest Stage of Originalism, 76 Chi.-Kent L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 103, 127\xe2\x80\x93128 (2000); Brief for Historians on Early American Legal, Constitutional, and Pennsylvania History as Amici Curiae 22\xe2\x80\x9333.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Further, there is no popular consensus that the private self-defense right described in Heller is fundamental. The plurality suggests that two amici briefs filed in the case show such a consensus, see ante, at 42\xe2\x80\x9343, but, of course, numerous amici briefs have been filed opposing incorporation as well. Moreover, every State regulates firearms extensively, and public opinion is sharply divided on the appropriate level of regulation. Much of this disagreement rests upon empirical considerations. One side believes the right essential to protect the lives of those attacked in the home; the other side believes it essential to regulate the right in order to protect the lives of others attacked with guns. It seems unlikely that definitive evidence will develop one way or the other. And the appropriate level of firearm regulation has thus long been, and continues to be, a hotly contested matter of political debate. See, e.g., Siegel, Dead or Alive: Originalism as Popular Constitutionalism in Heller, 122 Harv. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 191, 201\xe2\x80\x93246 (2008). (Numerous sources supporting arguments and data in Part II\xe2\x80\x93B can be found in the Appendix, infra.)

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Moreover, there is no reason here to believe that incorporation of the private self-defense right will further any other or broader constitutional objective. We are aware of no argument that gun-control regulations target or are passed with the purpose of targeting \xe2\x80\x9cdiscrete and insular minorities.\xe2\x80\x9d Carolene Products Co., supra, at 153, n.\xc2\xa04; see, e.g., ante, at 49\xe2\x80\x9351 (Stevens, J., dissenting). Nor will incorporation help to assure equal respect for individuals. Unlike the First Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s rights of free speech, free press, assembly, and petition, the private self-defense right does not comprise a necessary part of the democratic process that the Constitution seeks to establish. See, e.g., Whitney v. California, 274 U. S. 357, 377 (1927) (Brandeis, J., concurring). Unlike the First Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s religious protections, the Fourth Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s protection against unreasonable searches and seizures, the Fifth and Sixth Amendments\xe2\x80\x99 insistence upon fair criminal procedure, and the Eighth Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s protection against cruel and unusual punishments, the private self-defense right does not significantly seek to protect individuals who might otherwise suffer unfair or inhumane treatment at the hands of a majority. Unlike the protections offered by many of these same Amendments, it does not involve matters as to which judges possess a comparative expertise, by virtue of their close familiarity with the justice system and its operation. And, unlike the Fifth Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s insistence on just compensation, it does not involve a matter where a majority might unfairly seize for itself property belonging to a minority.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, incorporation of the right will work a significant disruption in the constitutional allocation of decisionmaking authority, thereby interfering with the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s ability to further its objectives.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0First, on any reasonable accounting, the incorporation of the right recognized in Heller would amount to a significant incursion on a traditional and important area of state concern, altering the constitutional relationship between the States and the Federal Government. Private gun regulation is the quintessential exercise of a State\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cpolice power\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94i.e., the power to \xe2\x80\x9cprotec[t] \xe2\x80\xa6 the lives, limbs, health, comfort, and quiet of all persons, and the protection of all property within the State,\xe2\x80\x9d by enacting \xe2\x80\x9call kinds of restraints and burdens\xe2\x80\x9d on both \xe2\x80\x9cpersons and property.\xe2\x80\x9d Slaughter-House Cases, 16 Wall. 36, 62 (1873) (internal quotation marks omitted). The Court has long recognized that the Constitution grants the States special authority to enact laws pursuant to this power. See, e.g., Medtronic, Inc. v. Lohr, 518 U. S. 470, 475 (1996) (noting that States have \xe2\x80\x9cgreat latitude\xe2\x80\x9d to use their police powers (internal quotation marks omitted)); Metropolitan Life Ins. Co. v. Massachusetts, 471 U. S. 724, 756 (1985). A decade ago, we wrote that there is \xe2\x80\x9cno better example of the police power\xe2\x80\x9d than \xe2\x80\x9cthe suppression of violent crime.\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Morrison, 529 U. S. 598, 618 (2000). And examples in which the Court has deferred to state legislative judgments in respect to the exercise of the police power are legion. See, e.g., Gonzales v. Oregon, 546 U. S. 243, 270 (2006) (assisted suicide); Washington v. Glucksberg, 521 U. S. 702, 721 (1997) (same); Berman v. Parker, 348 U. S. 26, 32 (1954) (\xe2\x80\x9cWe deal, in other words, with what traditionally has been known as the police power. An attempt to define its reach or trace its outer limits is fruitless \xe2\x80\xa6\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Second, determining the constitutionality of a particular state gun law requires finding answers to complex empirically based questions of a kind that legislatures are better able than courts to make. See, e.g., Los Angeles v. Alameda Books, Inc., 535 U. S. 425, 440 (2002) (plurality opinion); Turner Broadcasting System, Inc. v. FCC, 520 U. S. 180, 195\xe2\x80\x93196 (1997). And it may require this kind of analysis in virtually every case.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Government regulation of the right to bear arms normally embodies a judgment that the regulation will help save lives. The determination whether a gun regulation is constitutional would thus almost always require the weighing of the constitutional right to bear arms against the \xe2\x80\x9cprimary concern of every government\xe2\x80\x94a concern for the safety and indeed the lives of its citizens.\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Salerno, 481 U. S. 739, 755 (1987). With respect to other incorporated rights, this sort of inquiry is sometimes present. See, e.g., Brandenburg v. Ohio, 395 U. S. 444, 447 (1969) (per curiam) (free speech); Sherbert v. Verner, 374 U. S. 398, 403 (1963) (religion); Brigham City v. Stuart, 547 U. S. 398, 403\xe2\x80\x93404 (2006) (Fourth Amendment); New York v. Quarles, 467 U. S. 649, 655 (1984) (Fifth Amendment); Salerno, supra, at 755 (bail). But here, this inquiry\xe2\x80\x94calling for the fine tuning of protective rules\xe2\x80\x94is likely to be part of a daily judicial diet.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Given the competing interests, courts will have to try to answer empirical questions of a particularly difficult kind. Suppose, for example, that after a gun regulation\xe2\x80\x99s adoption the murder rate went up. Without the gun regulation would the murder rate have risen even faster? How is this conclusion affected by the local recession which has left numerous people unemployed? What about budget cuts that led to a downsizing of the police force? How effective was that police force to begin with? And did the regulation simply take guns from those who use them for lawful purposes without affecting their possession by criminals?

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Consider too that countless gun regulations of many shapes and sizes are in place in every State and in many local communities. Does the right to possess weapons for self-defense extend outside the home? To the car? To work? What sort of guns are necessary for self-defense? Handguns? Rifles? Semiautomatic weapons? When is a gun semi-automatic? Where are different kinds of weapons likely needed? Does time-of-day matter? Does the presence of a child in the house matter? Does the presence of a convicted felon in the house matter? Do police need special rules permitting patdowns designed to find guns? When do registration requirements become severe to the point that they amount to an unconstitutional ban? Who can possess guns and of what kind? Aliens? Prior drug offenders? Prior alcohol abusers? How would the right interact with a state or local government\xe2\x80\x99s ability to take special measures during, say, national security emergencies? As the questions suggest, state and local gun regulation can become highly complex, and these \xe2\x80\x9care only a few uncertainties that quickly come to mind.\xe2\x80\x9d Caperton v. A. T. Massey Coal Co., 556 U. S. ___, ___ (2009) (Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J., dissenting) (slip op., at 10).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The difficulty of finding answers to these questions is exceeded only by the importance of doing so. Firearms cause well over 60,000 deaths and injuries in the United States each year. Those who live in urban areas, police officers, women, and children, all may be particularly at risk. And gun regulation may save their lives. Some experts have calculated, for example, that Chicago\xe2\x80\x99s handgun ban has saved several hundred lives, perhaps close to 1,000, since it was enacted in 1983. Other experts argue that stringent gun regulations \xe2\x80\x9ccan help protect police officers operating on the front lines against gun violence,\xe2\x80\x9d have reduced homicide rates in Washington, D.\xc2\xa0C., and Baltimore, and have helped to lower New York\xe2\x80\x99s crime and homicide rates.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0At the same time, the opponents of regulation cast doubt on these studies. And who is right? Finding out may require interpreting studies that are only indirectly related to a particular regulatory statute, say one banning handguns in the home. Suppose studies find more accidents and suicides where there is a handgun in the home than where there is a long gun in the home or no gun at all? To what extent do such studies justify a ban? What if opponents of the ban put forth counter studies?

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In answering such questions judges cannot simply refer to judicial homilies, such as Blackstone\xe2\x80\x99s 18th-century perception that a man\xe2\x80\x99s home is his castle. See 4 Blackstone 223. Nor can the plurality so simply reject, by mere assertion, the fact that \xe2\x80\x9cincorporation will require judges to assess the costs and benefits of firearms restrictions.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 44. How can the Court assess the strength of the government\xe2\x80\x99s regulatory interests without addressing issues of empirical fact? How can the Court determine if a regulation is appropriately tailored without considering its impact? And how can the Court determine if there are less restrictive alternatives without considering what will happen if those alternatives are implemented?

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Perhaps the Court could lessen the difficulty of the mission it has created for itself by adopting a jurisprudential approach similar to the many state courts that administer a state constitutional right to bear arms. See infra, at 19\xe2\x80\x9320 (describing state approaches). But the Court has not yet done so. Cf. Heller, 544 U. S., at ___ (slip op., at 57\xe2\x80\x9364) (rejecting an \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98interest-balancing\xe2\x80\x99 approach\xe2\x80\x9d similar to that employed by the States); ante, at 44 (plurality opinion). Rather, the Court has haphazardly created a few simple rules, such as that it will not touch \xe2\x80\x9cprohibitions on the possession of firearms by felons and the mentally ill,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9claws forbidding the carrying of firearms in sensitive places such as schools and government buildings,\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9claws imposing conditions and qualifications on the commercial sale of arms.\xe2\x80\x9d Heller, 544 U. S., at ___ (slip op., at 54\xe2\x80\x9355); Ante, at 39 (plurality opinion). But why these rules and not others? Does the Court know that these regulations are justified by some special gun-related risk of death? In fact, the Court does not know. It has simply invented rules that sound sensible without being able to explain why or how Chicago\xe2\x80\x99s handgun ban is different.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The fact is that judges do not know the answers to the kinds of empirically based questions that will often determine the need for particular forms of gun regulation. Nor do they have readily available \xe2\x80\x9ctools\xe2\x80\x9d for finding and evaluating the technical material submitted by others. District Attorney\xe2\x80\x99s Office for Third Judicial Dist. v. Osborne, 557 U. S. ___, ___ (2009) (slip op., at 21); see also Turner Broadcasting, 520 U. S., at 195\xe2\x80\x93196. Judges cannot easily make empirically based predictions; they have no way to gather and evaluate the data required to see if such predictions are accurate; and the nature of litigation and concerns about stare decisis further make it difficult for judges to change course if predictions prove inaccurate. Nor can judges rely upon local community views and values when reaching judgments in circumstances where prediction is difficult because the basic facts are unclear or unknown.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0At the same time, there is no institutional need to send judges off on this \xe2\x80\x9cmission-almost-impossible.\xe2\x80\x9d Legislators are able to \xe2\x80\x9camass the stuff of actual experience and cull conclusions from it.\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Gainey, 380 U. S. 63, 67 (1965). They are far better suited than judges to uncover facts and to understand their relevance. And legislators, unlike Article III judges, can be held democratically responsible for their empirically based and value-laden conclusions. We have thus repeatedly affirmed our preference for \xe2\x80\x9clegislative not judicial solutions\xe2\x80\x9d to this kind of problem, see, e.g., Patsy v. Board of Regents of Fla., 457 U. S. 496, 513 (1982), just as we have repeatedly affirmed the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s preference for democratic solutions legislated by those whom the people elect.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In New State Ice Co. v. Liebmann, 285 U. S. 262, 310\xe2\x80\x93311 (1932), Justice Brandeis stated in dissent:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cSome people assert that our present plight is due, in part, to the limitations set by courts upon experimentation in the fields of social and economic science; and to the discouragement to which proposals for betterment there have been subjected otherwise. There must be power in the States and the Nation to remould, through experimentation, our economic practices and institutions to meet changing social and economic needs. I cannot believe that the framers of the Fourteenth Amendment, or the States which ratified it, intended to deprive us of the power to correct [the social problems we face].\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0There are 50 state legislatures. The fact that this Court may already have refused to take this wise advice with respect to Congress in Heller is no reason to make matters worse here.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Third, the ability of States to reflect local preferences and conditions\xe2\x80\x94both key virtues of federalism\xe2\x80\x94here has particular importance. The incidence of gun ownership varies substantially as between crowded cities and uncongested rural communities, as well as among the different geographic regions of the country. Thus, approximately 60% of adults who live in the relatively sparsely populated Western States of Alaska, Montana, and Wyoming report that their household keeps a gun, while fewer than 15% of adults in the densely populated Eastern States of Rhode Island, New Jersey, and Massachusetts say the same.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The nature of gun violence also varies as between rural communities and cities. Urban centers face significantly greater levels of firearm crime and homicide, while rural communities have proportionately greater problems with nonhomicide gun deaths, such as suicides and accidents. And idiosyncratic local factors can lead to two cities finding themselves in dramatically different circumstances: For example, in 2008, the murder rate was 40 times higher in New Orleans than it was in Lincoln, Nebraska.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It is thus unsurprising that States and local communities have historically differed about the need for gun regulation as well as about its proper level. Nor is it surprising that \xe2\x80\x9cprimarily, and historically,\xe2\x80\x9d the law has treated the exercise of police powers, including gun control, as \xe2\x80\x9cmatter[s] of local concern.\xe2\x80\x9d Medtronic, 518 U. S., at 475 (internal quotation marks omitted).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Fourth, although incorporation of any right removes decisions from the democratic process, the incorporation of this particular right does so without strong offsetting justification\xe2\x80\x94as the example of Oak Park\xe2\x80\x99s handgun ban helps to show. See Oak Park, Ill., Municipal Code, \xc2\xa727\xe2\x80\x932\xe2\x80\x931 (1995). Oak Park decided to ban handguns in 1983, after a local attorney was shot to death with a handgun that his assailant had smuggled into a courtroom in a blanket. Brief for Oak Park Citizens Committee for Handgun Control as Amicus Curiae 1, 21 (hereinafter Oak Park Brief). A citizens committee spent months gathering information about handguns. Id., at 21. It secured 6,000 signatures from community residents in support of a ban. Id., at 21\xe2\x80\x9322. And the village board enacted a ban into law. Id., at 22.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Subsequently, at the urging of ban opponents the Board held a community referendum on the matter. Ibid. The citizens committee argued strongly in favor of the ban. Id., at 22\xe2\x80\x9323. It pointed out that most guns owned in Oak Park were handguns and that handguns were misused more often than citizens used them in self-defense. Id., at 23. The ban opponents argued just as strongly to the contrary. Ibid. The public decided to keep the ban by a vote of 8,031 to 6,368. Ibid. And since that time, Oak Park now tells us, crime has decreased and the community has seen no accidental handgun deaths. Id., at 2.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Given the empirical and local value-laden nature of the questions that lie at the heart of the issue, why, in a Nation whose Constitution foresees democratic decisionmaking, is it so fundamental a matter as to require taking that power from the people? What is it here that the people did not know? What is it that a judge knows better?

\n

*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In sum, the police power, the superiority of legislative decisionmaking, the need for local decisionmaking, the comparative desirability of democratic decisionmaking, the lack of a manageable judicial standard, and the life-threatening harm that may flow from striking down regulations all argue against incorporation. Where the incorporation of other rights has been at issue, some of these problems have arisen. But in this instance all these problems are present, all at the same time, and all are likely to be present in most, perhaps nearly all, of the cases in which the constitutionality of a gun regulation is at issue. At the same time, the important factors that favor incorporation in other instances\xe2\x80\x94e.g., the protection of broader constitutional objectives\xe2\x80\x94are not present here. The upshot is that all factors militate against incorporation\xe2\x80\x94with the possible exception of historical factors.

\n

III

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I must, then, return to history. The plurality, in seeking to justify incorporation, asks whether the interests the Second Amendment protects are \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98deeply rooted in this Nation\xe2\x80\x99s history and tradition.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 19 (quoting Glucksberg, 521 U. S., at 721; internal quotation marks omitted). It looks to selected portions of the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s history for the answer. And it finds an affirmative reply.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As I have made clear, I do not believe history is the only pertinent consideration. Nor would I read history as broadly as the majority does. In particular, since we here are evaluating a more particular right\xe2\x80\x94namely, the right to bear arms for purposes of private self-defense\xe2\x80\x94general historical references to the \xe2\x80\x9cright to keep and bear arms\xe2\x80\x9d are not always helpful. Depending upon context, early historical sources may mean to refer to a militia-based right\xe2\x80\x94a matter of considerable importance 200 years ago\xe2\x80\x94which has, as the majority points out, \xe2\x80\x9clargely faded as a popular concern.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 22. There is no reason to believe that matters of such little contemporary importance should play a significant role in answering the incorporation question. See Apodaca, 406 U. S., at 410 (incorporation \xe2\x80\x9cinquiry must focus upon the function served\xe2\x80\x9d by the right in question in \xe2\x80\x9ccontemporary society\xe2\x80\x9d); Wolf v. Colorado, 338 U. S. 25, 27 (1949) (incorporation must take into account \xe2\x80\x9cthe movements of a free society\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cthe gradual and empiric process of inclusion and exclusion\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)); cf. U. S. Const., Art. I, \xc2\xa7910 (prohibiting federal officeholders from accepting a \xe2\x80\x9cTitle, of any kind whatever, from [a] foreign State\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94presumably a matter of considerable importance 200 years ago).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0That said, I can find much in the historical record that shows that some Americans in some places at certain times thought it important to keep and bear arms for private self-defense. For instance, the reader will see that many States have constitutional provisions protecting gun possession. But, as far as I can tell, those provisions typically do no more than guarantee that a gun regulation will be a reasonable police power regulation. See Winkler, Scrutinizing the Second Amendment, 105 Mich. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 683, 686, 716\xe2\x80\x93717 (2007) (the \xe2\x80\x9ccourts of every state to consider the question apply a deferential \xe2\x80\x98reasonable regulation\xe2\x80\x99 standard\xe2\x80\x9d) (hereinafter Winkler, Scrutinizing); see also id., at 716\xe2\x80\x93717 (explaining the difference between that standard and ordinary rational-basis review). It is thus altogether unclear whether such provisions would prohibit cities such as Chicago from enacting laws, such as the law before us, banning handguns. See id., at 723. The majority, however, would incorporate a right that is likely inconsistent with Chicago\xe2\x80\x99s law; and the majority would almost certainly strike down that law. Cf. Heller, 554 U. S., at ___ (slip op., at 57\xe2\x80\x9364) (striking down the District of Columbia\xe2\x80\x99s handgun ban).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Thus, the specific question before us is not whether there are references to the right to bear arms for self-defense throughout this Nation\xe2\x80\x99s history\xe2\x80\x94of course there are\xe2\x80\x94or even whether the Court should incorporate a simple constitutional requirement that firearms regulations not unreasonably burden the right to keep and bear arms, but rather whether there is a consensus that so substantial a private self-defense right as the one described in Heller applies to the States. See, e.g., Glucksberg, supra, at 721 (requiring \xe2\x80\x9ca careful description\xe2\x80\x9d of the right at issue when deciding whether it is \xe2\x80\x9cdeeply rooted in this Nation\xe2\x80\x99s history and tradition\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)). On this question, the reader will have to make up his or her own mind about the historical record that I describe in part below. In my view, that record is insufficient to say that the right to bear arms for private self-defense, as explicated by Heller, is fundamental in the sense relevant to the incorporation inquiry. As the evidence below shows, States and localities have consistently enacted firearms regulations, including regulations similar to those at issue here, throughout our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s history. Courts have repeatedly upheld such regulations. And it is, at the very least, possible, and perhaps likely, that incorporation will impose on every, or nearly every, State a different right to bear arms than they currently recognize\xe2\x80\x94a right that threatens to destabilize settled state legal principles. Cf. 554 U. S., at ___ (slip op., at 57\xe2\x80\x9364) (rejecting an \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98interest-balancing\xe2\x80\x99 approach\xe2\x80\x9d similar to that employed by the States).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I thus cannot find a historical consensus with respect to whether the right described by Heller is \xe2\x80\x9cfundamental\xe2\x80\x9d as our incorporation cases use that term. Nor can I find sufficient historical support for the majority\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion that that right is \xe2\x80\x9cdeeply rooted in this Nation\xe2\x80\x99s history and tradition.\xe2\x80\x9d Instead, I find no more than ambiguity and uncertainty that perhaps even expert historians would find difficult to penetrate. And a historical record that is so ambiguous cannot itself provide an adequate basis for incorporating a private right of self-defense and applying it against the States.

\n

\nThe Eighteenth Century

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The opinions in Heller collect much of the relevant 18th-century evidence. See 554 U. S., at ___ (slip op., at 5\xe2\x80\x9332); id., at ___ (Stevens, J., dissenting) (slip op., at 5\xe2\x80\x9331); id., at ___ (Breyer, J., dissenting) (slip op., at 4\xe2\x80\x937). In respect to the relevant question\xe2\x80\x94the \xe2\x80\x9cdeeply rooted nature\xe2\x80\x9d of a right to keep and bear arms for purposes of private self-defense\xe2\x80\x94that evidence is inconclusive, particularly when augmented as follows:

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0First, as I have noted earlier in this opinion, and Justice Stevens argued in dissent, the history discussed in Heller shows that the Second Amendment was enacted primarily for the purpose of protecting militia-related rights. See supra, at 4; Heller, supra, at ___ (slip op., at 5\xe2\x80\x9331). Many of the scholars and historians who have written on the subject apparently agree. See supra, at 2\xe2\x80\x935.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Second, historians now tell us that the right to which Blackstone referred, an important link in the Heller majority\xe2\x80\x99s historical argument, concerned the right of Parliament (representing the people) to form a militia to oppose a tyrant (the King) threatening to deprive the people of their traditional liberties (which did not include an unregulated right to possess guns). Thus, 18th-century language referring to a \xe2\x80\x9cright to keep and bear arms\xe2\x80\x9d does not ipso facto refer to a private right of self-defense\xe2\x80\x94certainly not unambiguously so. See English Historians\xe2\x80\x99 Brief 3\xe2\x80\x9327; see also supra, at 2\xe2\x80\x935.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Third, scholarly articles indicate that firearms were heavily regulated at the time of the framing\xe2\x80\x94perhaps more heavily regulated than the Court in Heller believed. For example, one scholar writes that \xe2\x80\x9c[h]undreds of individual statutes regulated the possession and use of guns in colonial and early national America.\xe2\x80\x9d Churchill, Gun Regulation, the Police Power, and the Right to Keep Arms, 25 Law & Hist. Rev. 139, 143 (2007). Among these statutes was a ban on the private firing of weapons in Boston, as well as comprehensive restrictions on similar conduct in Philadelphia and New York. See Acts and Laws of Massachusetts, p. 208 (1746); 5 J. Mitchell, & H. Flanders, Statutes at Large of Pennsylvania From 1682 to 1801, pp. 108\xe2\x80\x93109 (1898); 4 Colonial Laws of New York ch. 1233, p. 748 (1894); see also Churchill, supra, at 162\xe2\x80\x93163 (discussing bans on the shooting of guns in Pennsylvania and New York).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Fourth, after the Constitution was adopted, several States continued to regulate firearms possession by, for example, adopting rules that would have prevented the carrying of loaded firearms in the city, Heller, 554 U. S., at ___ (slip op., at 5\xe2\x80\x937) (Breyer, J., dissenting); see also id., at ___ (slip op., at 59\xe2\x80\x9360). Scholars have thus concluded that the primary Revolutionary era limitation on a State\xe2\x80\x99s police power to regulate guns appears to be only that regulations were \xe2\x80\x9caimed at a legitimate public purpose\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cconsistent with reason.\xe2\x80\x9d Cornell, Early American Gun Regulation and the Second Amendment, 25 Law & Hist. Rev. 197, 198 (2007).

\n

\nThe Pre-Civil War Nineteenth Century

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I would also augment the majority\xe2\x80\x99s account of this period as follows:

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0First, additional States began to regulate the discharge of firearms in public places. See, e.g., Act of Feb. 17, 1831, \xc2\xa76, reprinted in 3 Statutes of Ohio and the Northwestern Territory 1740 (S. Chase ed. 1835); Act of Dec. 3, 1825, ch. CCXCII, \xc2\xa73, 1825 Tenn. Priv. Acts 306.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Second, States began to regulate the possession of concealed weapons, which were both popular and dangerous. See, e.g., C. Cramer, Concealed Weapon Laws of the Early Republic 143\xe2\x80\x93152 (1999) (collecting examples); see also 1837\xe2\x80\x931838 Tenn. Pub. Acts ch. 137, pp. 200\xe2\x80\x93201 (banning the wearing, sale, or giving of Bowie knives); 1847 Va. Acts ch. 7, \xc2\xa78, p. 110, (\xe2\x80\x9cAny free person who shall habitually carry about his person, hidden from common observation, any pistol, dirk, bowie knife, or weapon of the like kind, from the use of which the death of any person might probably ensue, shall for every offense be punished by [a] fine not exceed fifty dollars\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0State courts repeatedly upheld the validity of such laws, finding that, even when the state constitution granted a right to bear arms, the legislature was permitted to, e.g., \xe2\x80\x9cabolish\xe2\x80\x9d these small, inexpensive, \xe2\x80\x9cmost dangerous weapons entirely from use,\xe2\x80\x9d even in self-defense. Day v. State, 37 Tenn. 496, 500 (1857); see also, e.g., State v. Jumel, 13 La. Ann. 399, 400 (1858) (upholding concealed weapon ban because it \xe2\x80\x9cprohibited only a particular mode of bearing arms which is found dangerous to the peace of society\xe2\x80\x9d); State v. Chandler, 5 La. Ann. 489, 489\xe2\x80\x93490 (1850) (upholding concealed weapon ban and describing the law as \xe2\x80\x9cabsolutely necessary to counteract a vicious state of society, growing out of the habit of carrying concealed weapons\xe2\x80\x9d); State v. Reid, 1 Ala. 612, 616\xe2\x80\x93617 (1840).

\n

\nThe Post-Civil War Nineteenth Century

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It is important to read the majority\xe2\x80\x99s account with the following considerations in mind:

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0First, the Court today properly declines to revisit our interpretation of the Privileges or Immunities Clause. See ante, at 10. The Court\xe2\x80\x99s case for incorporation must thus rest on the conclusion that the right to bear arms is \xe2\x80\x9cfundamental.\xe2\x80\x9d But the very evidence that it advances in support of the conclusion that Reconstruction-era Americans strongly supported a private self-defense right shows with equal force that Americans wanted African-American citizens to have the same rights to possess guns as did white citizens. Ante, at 22\xe2\x80\x9333. Here, for example is what Congress said when it enacted a Fourteenth Amendment predecessor, the Second Freedman\xe2\x80\x99s Bureau Act. It wrote that the statute, in order to secure \xe2\x80\x9cthe constitutional right to bear arms \xe2\x80\xa6 for all citizens,\xe2\x80\x9d would assure that each citizen:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cshall have \xe2\x80\xa6 full and equal benefit of all laws and proceedings concerning personal liberty, personal security, and the acquisition, enjoyment, and disposition of estate, real and personal, including the constitutional right to bear arms, [by securing] \xe2\x80\xa6 to \xe2\x80\xa6 all the citizens of [every] \xe2\x80\xa6 State or district without respect to race or color, or previous condition of slavery.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa714, 14 Stat. 176\xe2\x80\x93177 (emphasis added).

\n

\nThis sounds like an antidiscrimination provision. See Rosenthal, The New Originalism Meets the Fourteenth Amendment: Original Public Meaning and the Problem of Incorporation, 18 J. Contemp. Legal Issues 361, 383\xe2\x80\x93384 (2009) (discussing evidence that the Freedmen\xe2\x80\x99s Bureau was focused on discrimination).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Another Fourteenth Amendment predecessor, the Civil Rights Act of 1866, also took aim at discrimination. See \xc2\xa71, 14 Stat. 27 (citizens of \xe2\x80\x9cevery race and color, without regard to any previous condition of slavery or involuntary servitude . . . shall have the same right [to engage in various activities] and to full and equal benefit of all laws \xe2\x80\xa6 as is enjoyed by white citizens\xe2\x80\x9d). And, of course, the Fourteenth Amendment itself insists that all States guarantee their citizens the \xe2\x80\x9cequal protection of the laws.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0There is thus every reason to believe that the fundamental concern of the Reconstruction Congress was the eradication of discrimination, not the provision of a new substantive right to bear arms free from reasonable state police power regulation. See, e.g., Brief for Municipal Respondents 62\xe2\x80\x9369 (discussing congressional record evidence that Reconstruction Congress was concerned about discrimination). Indeed, why would those who wrote the Fourteenth Amendment have wanted to give such a right to Southerners who had so recently waged war against the North, and who continued to disarm and oppress recently freed African-American citizens? Cf. Act of Mar. 2, 1867, \xc2\xa76, 14 Stat. 487 (disbanding Southern militias because they were, inter alia, disarming the freedmen).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Second, firearms regulation in the later part of the 19th century was common. The majority is correct that the Freedmen\xe2\x80\x99s Bureau points to a right to bear arms, and it stands to reason, as the majority points out, that \xe2\x80\x9c[i]t would have been nonsensical for Congress to guarantee the \xe2\x80\xa6 equal benefit of a \xe2\x80\xa6 right that does not exist.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 32. But the majority points to no evidence that there existed during this period a fundamental right to bear arms for private self-defense immune to the reasonable exercise of the state police power. See Emberton, The Limits of Incorporation: Violence, Gun Rights, and Gun Regulation in the Reconstruction South, 17 Stan. L. & Pol\xe2\x80\x99y Rev. 615, 621\xe2\x80\x93622 (2006) (noting that history shows that \xe2\x80\x9cnineteenth-century Americans\xe2\x80\x9d were \xe2\x80\x9cnot opposed to the idea that the state should be able to control the use of firearms\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0To the contrary, in the latter half of the 19th century, a number of state constitutions adopted or amended after the Civil War explicitly recognized the legislature\xe2\x80\x99s general ability to limit the right to bear arms. See Tex. Const., Art. I, \xc2\xa713 (1869) (protecting \xe2\x80\x9cthe right to keep and bear arms,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cunder such regulations as the legislature may prescribe\xe2\x80\x9d); Idaho Const., Art. I, \xc2\xa711 (1889) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe people have the right to bear arms \xe2\x80\xa6; but the Legislature shall regulate the exercise of this right by law\xe2\x80\x9d); Utah Const., Art. I, \xc2\xa76 (1896) (same). And numerous other state constitutional provisions adopted during this period explicitly granted the legislature various types of regulatory power over firearms. See Brief for Thirty-Four Professional Historians et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae 14\xe2\x80\x9315 (hereinafter Legal Historians\xe2\x80\x99 Brief).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Moreover, four States largely banned the possession of all nonmilitary handguns during this period. See 1879 Tenn. Pub. Acts ch. 186, \xc2\xa71 (prohibiting citizens from carrying \xe2\x80\x9cpublicly or privately, any \xe2\x80\xa6 belt or pocket pistol, revolver, or any kind of pistol, except the army or navy pistol, usually used in warfare, which shall be carried openly in the hand\xe2\x80\x9d); 1876 Wyo. Comp. Laws ch. 52, \xc2\xa71 (forbidding \xe2\x80\x9cconcealed or ope[n]\xe2\x80\x9d bearing of \xe2\x80\x9cany fire arm or other deadly weapon, within the limits of any city, town or village\xe2\x80\x9d); Ark. Act of Apr. 1, 1881, ch. 96, \xc2\xa71 (prohibiting the \xe2\x80\x9cwear[ing] or carry[ng]\xe2\x80\x9d of \xe2\x80\x9cany pistol \xe2\x80\xa6 except such pistols as are used in the army or navy,\xe2\x80\x9d except while traveling or at home); Tex. Act of Apr. 12, 1871, ch. 34 (prohibiting the carrying of pistols unless there are \xe2\x80\x9cimmediate and pressing\xe2\x80\x9d reasonable grounds to fear \xe2\x80\x9cimmediate and pressing\xe2\x80\x9d attack or for militia service). Fifteen States banned the concealed carry of pistols and other deadly weapons. See Legal Historians\xe2\x80\x99 Brief 16, n.\xc2\xa014. And individual municipalities enacted stringent gun controls, often in response to local conditions\xe2\x80\x94Dodge City, Kansas, for example, joined many western cattle towns in banning the carrying of pistols and other dangerous weapons in response to violence accompanying western cattle drives. See Brief for Municipal Respondents 30 (citing Dodge City, Kan., Ordinance No. 16, \xc2\xa7XI (Sept. 22, 1876)); D. Courtwright, The Cowboy Subculture, in Guns in America: A Reader 96 (J. Dizard et\xc2\xa0al. eds. 1999) (discussing how Western cattle towns required cowboys to \xe2\x80\x9ccheck\xe2\x80\x9d their guns upon entering town).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Further, much as they had during the period before the Civil War, state courts routinely upheld such restrictions. See, e.g., English v. State, 35 Tex. 473 (1871); Hill v. State, 53 Ga. 472, 475 (1874); Fife v. State, 31 Ark. 455, 461 (1876); State v. Workman, 35 W.\xc2\xa0Va. 367, 373 (1891). The Tennessee Supreme Court, in upholding a ban on possession of nonmilitary handguns and certain other weapons, summarized the Reconstruction understanding of the states\xe2\x80\x99 police power to regulate firearms:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cAdmitting the right of self-defense in its broadest sense, still on sound principle every good citizen is bound to yield his preference as to the means to be used, to the demands of the public good; and where certain weapons are forbidden to be kept or used by the law of the land, in order to the prevention of [sic] crime\xe2\x80\x94a great public end\xe2\x80\x94no man can be permitted to disregard this general end, and demand of the community the right, in order to gratify his whim or willful desire to use a particular weapon in his particular self-defense. The law allows ample means of self-defense, without the use of the weapons which we have held may be rightfully prescribed by this statute. The object being to banish these weapons from the community by an absolute prohibition for the prevention of crime, no man\xe2\x80\x99s particular safety, if such case could exist, ought to be allowed to defeat this end.\xe2\x80\x9d Andrews v. State, 50 Tenn. 165, 188\xe2\x80\x93189 (1871) (emphasis added).

\n

\nThe Twentieth and Twenty-First Centuries

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Although the majority does not discuss 20th- or 21st-century evidence concerning the Second Amendment at any length, I think that it is essential to consider the recent history of the right to bear arms for private self-defense when considering whether the right is \xe2\x80\x9cfundamental.\xe2\x80\x9d To that end, many States now provide state constitutional protection for an individual\xe2\x80\x99s right to keep and bear arms. See Volokh, State Constitutional Rights to Keep and Bear Arms, 11 Tex. Rev. L. & Pol. 191, 205 (2006) (identifying over 40 States). In determining the importance of this fact, we should keep the following considerations in mind:

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0First, by the end of the 20th century, in every State and many local communities, highly detailed and complicated regulatory schemes governed (and continue to govern) nearly every aspect of firearm ownership: Who may sell guns and how they must be sold; who may purchase guns and what type of guns may be purchased; how firearms must be stored and where they may be used; and so on. See generally Legal Community Against Violence, Regulating Guns In America (2008), available at http://\nwww.lcav.org/publications-briefs/regulating_guns. asp (all Internet materials as visited June 24, 2010, and available in Clerk of Court\xe2\x80\x99s case file) (detailing various arms regulations in every State).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Of particular relevance here, some municipalities ban handguns, even in States that constitutionally protect the right to bear arms. See Chicago, Ill., Municipal Code, \xc2\xa78\xe2\x80\x9320\xe2\x80\x93050(c) (2009); Oak Park, Ill., Municipal Code, \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa727\xe2\x80\x932\xe2\x80\x931, 27\xe2\x80\x931\xe2\x80\x931 (1995); Toledo, Ohio, Municipal Code, ch. 549.25 (2010). Moreover, at least seven States and Puerto Rico ban assault weapons or semiautomatic weapons. See Cal. Penal Code Ann. \xc2\xa712280(b) (West Supp. 2009); Conn. Gen. Stat. Ann. \xc2\xa753\xe2\x80\x93202c (2007); Haw. Rev. Stat. \xc2\xa7134\xe2\x80\x938 (1993); Md. Crim. Law Code Ann. \xc2\xa74\xe2\x80\x93303(a) (Lexis 2002); Mass. Gen. Laws, ch. 140, \xc2\xa7131M (West 2006); N.\xc2\xa0J. Stat. Ann. \xc2\xa72C:39-5 (West Supp. 2010); N. Y. Penal Law Ann. \xc2\xa7265.02(7) (West Supp. 2008); 25 Laws P. R. Ann. \xc2\xa7456m (Supp. 2006); see also 18 U. S. C. \xc2\xa7922(o) (federal machinegun ban).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Thirteen municipalities do the same. See Albany, N. Y., City Code \xc2\xa7193\xe2\x80\x9316(A) (2005); Aurora, Ill., Code of Ordinances \xc2\xa729\xe2\x80\x9349(a) (2009); Buffalo, N. Y., City Code \xc2\xa7180\xe2\x80\x931(F) (2000); Chicago, Ill., Municipal Code \xc2\xa78\xe2\x80\x9324\xe2\x80\x93025(a) (2010); Cincinnati, Ohio, Municipal Code \xc2\xa7708\xe2\x80\x9337(a) (2008); Cleveland, Ohio, Codified Ordinances \xc2\xa7628.03(a) (2008); Columbus, Ohio, City Code \xc2\xa72323.31 (2007); Denver, Colo., Municipal Code \xc2\xa738\xe2\x80\x93130(e) (2008); Morton Grove, Ill., Village Code \xc2\xa76\xe2\x80\x932\xe2\x80\x933(A); N. Y. C. Admin. Code \xc2\xa710\xe2\x80\x93303.1 (2009); Oak Park, Ill., Village Code \xc2\xa727\xe2\x80\x932\xe2\x80\x931 (2009); Rochester, N. Y., City Code \xc2\xa747\xe2\x80\x935(F) (2008); Toledo, Ohio, Municipal Code \xc2\xa7549.23(a). And two States, Maryland and Hawaii, ban assault pistols. See Haw. Rev. Stat. Ann. \xc2\xa7134\xe2\x80\x938; Md. Crim. Law Code Ann. \xc2\xa74\xe2\x80\x93303 (Lexis 2002).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Second, as I stated earlier, state courts in States with constitutions that provide gun rights have almost uniformly interpreted those rights as providing protection only against unreasonable regulation of guns. See, e.g., Winkler, Scrutinizing 686 (the \xe2\x80\x9ccourts of every state to consider\xe2\x80\x9d a gun regulation apply the \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98reasonable regulation\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d approach); State v. McAdams, 714 P.\xc2\xa02d 1236, 1238 (Wyo. 1986); Robertson v. City & County of Denver, 874 P.\xc2\xa02d 325, 328 (Colo. 1994).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0When determining reasonableness those courts have normally adopted a highly deferential attitude towards legislative determinations. See Winkler, Scrutinizing 723 (identifying only six cases in the 60 years before the article\xe2\x80\x99s publication striking down gun control laws: three that banned \xe2\x80\x9cthe transportation of any firearms for any purpose whatsoever,\xe2\x80\x9d a single \xe2\x80\x9cpermitting law,\xe2\x80\x9d and two as-applied challenges in \xe2\x80\x9cunusual circumstances\xe2\x80\x9d). Hence, as evidenced by the breadth of existing regulations, States and local governments maintain substantial flexibility to regulate firearms\xe2\x80\x94much as they seemingly have throughout the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s history\xe2\x80\x94even in those States with an arms right in their constitutions.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0 Although one scholar implies that state courts are less willing to permit total gun prohibitions, see Volokh, Implementing the Right to Keep and Bear Arms for Self-Defense: An Analytical Framework and a Research Agenda, 56 UCLA L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 1443, 1458 (2009), I am aware of no instances in the past 50 years in which a state court has struck down as unconstitutional a law banning a particular class of firearms, see Winkler, Scrutinizing 723.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Indeed, state courts have specifically upheld as constitutional (under their state constitutions) firearms regulations that have included handgun bans. See Kalodimos v. Village of Morton Grove, 103 Ill. 2d 483, 499, 470 N.\xc2\xa0E. 2d 266, 273 (1984) (upholding a handgun ban because the arms right is merely a right \xe2\x80\x9cto possess some form of weapon suitable for self-defense or recreation\xe2\x80\x9d); Cleveland v. Turner, No. 36126, 1977 WL 201393, *5 (Ohio Ct. App., Aug. 4, 1977) (handgun ban \xe2\x80\x9cdoes not absolutely interfere with the right of the people to bear arms, but rather proscribes possession of a specifically defined category of handguns\xe2\x80\x9d); State v. Bolin 378 S.\xc2\xa0C. 96, 99, 662 S.\xc2\xa0E. 2d 38, 39 (2008) (ban on handgun possession by persons under 21 did not infringe arms right because they can \xe2\x80\x9cposses[s] other types of guns\xe2\x80\x9d). Thus, the majority\xe2\x80\x99s decision to incorporate the private self-defense right recognized in Heller threatens to alter state regulatory regimes, at least as they pertain to handguns.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Third, the plurality correctly points out that only a few state courts, a \xe2\x80\x9cpaucity\xe2\x80\x9d of state courts, have specifically upheld handgun bans. Ante, at 39. But which state courts have struck them down? The absence of supporting information does not help the majority find support. Cf. United States v. Wells, 519 U. S. 482, 496 (1997) (noting that it is \xe2\x80\x9ctreacherous to find in congressional silence alone the adoption of a controlling rule of law\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)). Silence does not show or tend to show a consensus that a private self-defense right (strong enough to strike down a handgun ban) is \xe2\x80\x9cdeeply rooted in this Nation\xe2\x80\x99s history and tradition.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In sum, the Framers did not write the Second Amendment in order to protect a private right of armed self-defense. There has been, and is, no consensus that the right is, or was, \xe2\x80\x9cfundamental.\xe2\x80\x9d No broader constitutional interest or principle supports legal treatment of that right as fundamental. To the contrary, broader constitutional concerns of an institutional nature argue strongly against that treatment.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Moreover, nothing in 18th-, 19th-, 20th-, or 21st-century history shows a consensus that the right to private armed self-defense, as described in Heller, is \xe2\x80\x9cdeeply rooted in this Nation\xe2\x80\x99s history or tradition\xe2\x80\x9d or is otherwise \xe2\x80\x9cfundamental.\xe2\x80\x9d Indeed, incorporating the right recognized in Heller may change the law in many of the 50 States. Read in the majority\xe2\x80\x99s favor, the historical evidence is at most ambiguous. And, in the absence of any other support for its conclusion, ambiguous history cannot show that the Fourteenth Amendment incorporates a private right of self-defense against the States.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0With respect, I dissent.

\n

\n
\n\n
\nSTEVENS, J., DISSENTING
\nMCDONALD V. CHICAGO
\n561 U. S. ____ (2010)
\nSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES
\nNO. 08-1521
\n
\n

OTIS McDONALD, et\xc2\xa0al., PETITIONERS v. CITY OF CHICAGO, ILLINOIS, et\xc2\xa0al.

\n

on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the seventh circuit

\n

[June 28, 2010]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Stevens, dissenting.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In District of Columbia v. Heller, 554 U. S. ___, ___ (2008) (slip op., at 1), the Court answered the question whether a federal enclave\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cprohibition on the possession of usable handguns in the home violates the Second Amendment to the Constitution.\xe2\x80\x9d The question we should be answering in this case is whether the Constitution \xe2\x80\x9cguarantees individuals a fundamental right,\xe2\x80\x9d enforceable against the States, \xe2\x80\x9cto possess a functional, personal firearm, including a handgun, within the home.\xe2\x80\x9d Complaint \xc2\xb634, App. 23. That is a different\xe2\x80\x94and more difficult\xe2\x80\x94inquiry than asking if the Fourteenth Amendment \xe2\x80\x9cincorporates\xe2\x80\x9d the Second Amendment. The so-called incorporation question was squarely and, in my view, correctly resolved in the late 19th century.[Footnote 1]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Before the District Court, petitioners focused their pleadings on the special considerations raised by domestic possession, which they identified as the core of their asserted right. In support of their claim that the city of Chicago\xe2\x80\x99s handgun ban violates the Constitution, they now rely primarily on the Privileges or Immunities Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. See Brief for Petitioners 9\xe2\x80\x9365. They rely secondarily on the Due Process Clause of that Amendment. See id., at 66\xe2\x80\x9372. Neither submission requires the Court to express an opinion on whether the Fourteenth Amendment places any limit on the power of States to regulate possession, use, or carriage of firearms outside the home.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I agree with the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s refusal to accept petitioners\xe2\x80\x99 primary submission. Ante, at 10. Their briefs marshal an impressive amount of historical evidence for their argument that the Court interpreted the Privileges or Immunities Clause too narrowly in the Slaughter-House Cases, 16 Wall. 36 (1873). But the original meaning of the Clause is not as clear as they suggest[Footnote 2]\xe2\x80\x94and not nearly as clear as it would need to be to dislodge 137 years of precedent. The burden is severe for those who seek radical change in such an established body of constitutional doctrine.[Footnote 3] Moreover, the suggestion that invigorating the Privileges or Immunities Clause will reduce judicial discretion, see Reply Brief for Petitioners 22, n.\xc2\xa08, 26; Tr. of Oral Arg. 64\xe2\x80\x9365, strikes me as implausible, if not exactly backwards. \xe2\x80\x9cFor the very reason that it has so long remained a clean slate, a revitalized Privileges or Immunities Clause holds special hazards for judges who are mindful that their proper task is not to write their personal views of appropriate public policy into the Constitution.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 4]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I further agree with the plurality that there are weighty arguments supporting petitioners\xe2\x80\x99 second submission, insofar as it concerns the possession of firearms for lawful self-defense in the home. But these arguments are less compelling than the plurality suggests; they are much less compelling when applied outside the home; and their validity does not depend on the Court\xe2\x80\x99s holding in Heller. For that holding sheds no light on the meaning of the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. Our decisions construing that Clause to render various procedural guarantees in the Bill of Rights enforceable against the States likewise tell us little about the meaning of the word \xe2\x80\x9cliberty\xe2\x80\x9d in the Clause or about the scope of its protection of nonprocedural rights.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This is a substantive due process case.

\n

I

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Section 1 of the Fourteenth Amendment decrees that no State shall \xe2\x80\x9cdeprive any person of life, liberty, or property, without due process of law.\xe2\x80\x9d The Court has filled thousands of pages expounding that spare text. As I read the vast corpus of substantive due process opinions, they confirm several important principles that ought to guide our resolution of this case. The principal opinion\xe2\x80\x99s lengthy summary of our \xe2\x80\x9cincorporation\xe2\x80\x9d doctrine, see ante, at 5\xe2\x80\x939, 11\xe2\x80\x9319 (majority opinion), 10\xe2\x80\x9311 (plurality opinion), and its implicit (and untenable) effort to wall off that doctrine from the rest of our substantive due process jurisprudence, invite a fresh survey of this old terrain.

\n

Substantive Content

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The first, and most basic, principle established by our cases is that the rights protected by the Due Process Clause are not merely procedural in nature. At first glance, this proposition might seem surprising, given that the Clause refers to \xe2\x80\x9cprocess.\xe2\x80\x9d But substance and procedure are often deeply entwined. Upon closer inspection, the text can be read to \xe2\x80\x9cimpos[e] nothing less than an obligation to give substantive content to the words \xe2\x80\x98liberty\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98due process of law,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Washington v. Glucksberg, 521 U. S. 702, 764 (1997) (Souter, J., concurring in judgment), lest superficially fair procedures be permitted to \xe2\x80\x9cdestroy the enjoyment\xe2\x80\x9d of life, liberty, and property, Poe v. Ullman, 367 U. S. 497, 541 (1961) (Harlan, J., dissenting), and the Clause\xe2\x80\x99s prepositional modifier be permitted to swallow its primary command. Procedural guarantees are hollow unless linked to substantive interests; and no amount of process can legitimize some deprivations.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I have yet to see a persuasive argument that the Framers of the Fourteenth Amendment thought otherwise. To the contrary, the historical evidence suggests that, at least by the time of the Civil War if not much earlier, the phrase \xe2\x80\x9cdue process of law\xe2\x80\x9d had acquired substantive content as a term of art within the legal community.[Footnote 5] This understanding is consonant with the venerable \xe2\x80\x9cnotion that governmental authority has implied limits which preserve private autonomy,\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 6] a notion which predates the founding and which finds reinforcement in the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s Ninth Amendment, see Griswold v. Connecticut, 381 U. S. 479, 486\xe2\x80\x93493 (1965) (Goldberg, J., concurring).[Footnote 7] The Due Process Clause cannot claim to be the source of our basic freedoms\xe2\x80\x94no legal document ever could, see Meachum v. Fano, 427 U. S. 215, 230 (1976) (Stevens, J., dissenting)\xe2\x80\x94but it stands as one of their foundational guarantors in our law.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0If text and history are inconclusive on this point, our precedent leaves no doubt: It has been \xe2\x80\x9csettled\xe2\x80\x9d for well over a century that the Due Process Clause \xe2\x80\x9capplies to matters of substantive law as well as to matters of procedure.\xe2\x80\x9d Whitney v. California, 274 U. S. 357, 373 (1927) (Brandeis, J., concurring). Time and again, we have recognized that in the Fourteenth Amendment as well as the Fifth, the \xe2\x80\x9cDue Process Clause guarantees more than fair process, and the \xe2\x80\x98liberty\xe2\x80\x99 it protects includes more than the absence of physical restraint.\xe2\x80\x9d Glucksberg, 521 U. S., at 719. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Clause also includes a substantive component that \xe2\x80\x98provides heightened protection against government interference with certain fundamental rights and liberty interests.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Troxel v. Granville, 530 U. S. 57, 65 (2000) (opinion of O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, J., joined by Rehnquist, C.\xc2\xa0J., and Ginsburg and Breyer, JJ.) (quoting Glucksberg, 521 U. S., at 720). Some of our most enduring precedents, accepted today by virtually everyone, were substantive due process decisions. See, e.g., Loving v. Virginia, 388 U. S. 1, 12 (1967) (recognizing due-process- as well as equal-protection-based right to marry person of another race); Bolling v. Sharpe, 347 U. S. 497, 499\xe2\x80\x93500 (1954) (outlawing racial segregation in District of Columbia public schools); Pierce v. Society of Sisters, 268 U. S. 510, 534\xe2\x80\x93535 (1925) (vindicating right of parents to direct upbringing and education of their children); Meyer v. Nebraska, 262 U. S. 390, 399\xe2\x80\x93403 (1923) (striking down prohibition on teaching of foreign languages).

\n

\nLiberty

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The second principle woven through our cases is that substantive due process is fundamentally a matter of personal liberty. For it is the liberty clause of the Fourteenth Amendment that grounds our most important holdings in this field. It is the liberty clause that enacts the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cpromise\xe2\x80\x9d that a measure of dignity and self-rule will be afforded to all persons. Planned Parenthood of Southeastern Pa. v. Casey, 505 U. S. 833, 847 (1992). It is the liberty clause that reflects and renews \xe2\x80\x9cthe origins of the American heritage of freedom [and] the abiding interest in individual liberty that makes certain state intrusions on the citizen\xe2\x80\x99s right to decide how he will live his own life intolerable.\xe2\x80\x9d Fitzgerald v. Porter Memorial Hospital, 523 F.\xc2\xa02d 716, 720 (CA7 1975) (Stevens, J.). Our substantive due process cases have episodically invoked values such as privacy and equality as well, values that in certain contexts may intersect with or complement a subject\xe2\x80\x99s liberty interests in profound ways. But as I have observed on numerous occasions, \xe2\x80\x9cmost of the significant [20th-century] cases raising Bill of Rights issues have, in the final analysis, actually interpreted the word \xe2\x80\x98liberty\xe2\x80\x99 in the Fourteenth Amendment.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 8]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It follows that the term \xe2\x80\x9cincorporation,\xe2\x80\x9d like the term \xe2\x80\x9cunenumerated rights,\xe2\x80\x9d is something of a misnomer. Whether an asserted substantive due process interest is explicitly named in one of the first eight Amendments to the Constitution or is not mentioned, the underlying inquiry is the same: We must ask whether the interest is \xe2\x80\x9ccomprised within the term liberty.\xe2\x80\x9d Whitney, 274 U. S., at 373 (Brandeis, J., concurring). As the second Justice Harlan has shown, ever since the Court began considering the applicability of the Bill of Rights to the States, \xe2\x80\x9cthe Court\xe2\x80\x99s usual approach has been to ground the prohibitions against state action squarely on due process, without intermediate reliance on any of the first eight Amendments.\xe2\x80\x9d Malloy v. Hogan, 378 U. S. 1, 24 (1964) (dissenting opinion); see also Frankfurter, Memorandum on \xe2\x80\x9cIncorporation\xe2\x80\x9d of the Bill of Rights into the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment, 78 Harv. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 746, 747\xe2\x80\x93750 (1965). In the pathmarking case of Gitlow v. New York, 268 U. S. 652, 666 (1925), for example, both the majority and dissent evaluated petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s free speech claim not under the First Amendment but as an aspect of \xe2\x80\x9cthe fundamental personal rights and \xe2\x80\x98liberties\xe2\x80\x99 protected by the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment from impairment by the States.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 9]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In his own classic opinion in Griswold, 381 U.
